{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}camelCase\endSeq{}\endNode{}. It is a
          significant difference from C and Java, for example. In ooc, an
          integer is declared as \Node%
{\def\Element%
{41}}\Seq%

{5\p@}\def\saConditional%
{1}}The justification for that is consistency. In Java, for
          example, primitive types are in lowercase (\Node%
{\def\Element%
{44}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}int\endSeq{}\endNode{},
          \Node%
{\def\Element%
{45}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}byte\endSeq{}\endNode{}, \Node%
{\def\Element%
{46}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}short\endSeq{}\endNode{}) and classes are
          in \Node%
{\def\Element%
{47}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}CamelCase\endSeq{}\endNode{} (\Node%
{\def\Element%
{48}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}String\endSeq{}\endNode{},
          \Node%
{\def\Element%
{49}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}System\endSeq{}\endNode{}). There is some kind of consistency in
          this model, ie. you clearly see what is low-\/level and high-\/level.
          However, ooc takes another approach of consistency. Implementation
          details shouldn't interfere with the formatting of the source code.
          Hence, no distinction is made between an \Node%
{\def\Element%
{50}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}Int\endSeq{}\endNode{} and
          e.g. a \Node%
{\def\Element%
{51}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}MyClass\endSeq{}\endNode{}, from a user point of view.\endPar{}\endNode{}\endSeq{}\endDisplayGroup{}\endNode{}\Node%

{1}}If you try to declare something as 'int i', you'll be greeted
          by the compiler with the error message 'Invalid type name int. Types
          must start with an upper-\/case letter'. In this case, you can sort it
          out by using the ooc-\/ish 'Int'. But sometimes you just can't, e.g.
          with GLib types, '\Node%

{9\p@}}guint\endSeq{}\endNode{}', etc. The correct handling of these cases
          is to \Node%

{183}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}typedef\endSeq{}\endNode{}' them to something
          capitalized.\endPar{}\endNode{}\Node%

{1}}A cover is a set of functions added to a primitive type. For
        example, String is a typedef to char*, and it has a cover
        (\Node%
{\def\Element%
{201}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}lang.String\endSeq{}\endNode{}), which adds functions
        \Node%
{\def\Element%
{202}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}length\endSeq{}\endNode{}, \Node%
{\def\Element%
{203}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}equals\endSeq{}\endNode{},
        \Node%
{\def\Element%
{204}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}startsWith\endSeq{}\endNode{}, etc. Covers are low-\/level constructs
        with a high-\/level syntax, which makes them very useful in
        performance-\/critical environments.\endPar{}\endNode{}\Node%

{1}}import~structs.Array;

func~main(Int~argc,~String[]~argv)~\{

~~for(Int~arg:~new~Array(argc,~argv))~\{
~~~~printf("Got~arg~'\%s'\char92{}n",~arg);
~~\}

~~Array~strings~=~new~Array(4);
~~strings.set(0,~"Hai~furry~world!\char92{}n");~//~okay,~0~>\/=~0~and~0~<\/~4
~~strings.get(9);~//~ERROR!~index~out~of~bounds~(9~>\/~4).

{5\p@}\def\sbMin%

{5\p@}\def\sbConditional%
{1}\def\saNom%
{5\p@}\def\saMin%
{5\p@}\def\saMax%
{5\p@}\def\saConditional%
{1}}Pointers are used in ooc exactly as in C, although they are not as
      welcome. They're used extensively behind the scenes, to implement
      classes, etc, but their use is discouraged when not necessary. A good
      use case for using pointers (instead of a objects, for example) is
      low-\/level implementation of a speed-\/critical algorithm. Using pointers
      is to ooc what assembler is to C: something you shouldn't use unless you
      really need it for speed.\endPar{}\endNode{}\endSeq{}\endDisplayGroup{}\endNode{}\endSeq{}\Seq%

{1}}/*
~*~In~this~example,~we~use~SDL~functions~to~demonstrate~that
~*~you~have~to~use~parenthesis~to~call~C~functions.
~*~If~you~were~to~use~the~SDL~in~a~real~project,~it'd~be~better~to~use~the~SDL~bindings
~*/
include~SDL/SDL;

func~main~\{
~~init;~//~Need~no~parenthesis~!
~~quit;
\}

func~init~\{
~~SDL\char95{}Init(SDL\char95{}INIT\char95{}EVERYTHING);
\}

func~quit~\{
~~SDL\char95{}Quit();~//~We~must~use~parenthesis,~cause~it's~a~C~function
\}\endPar{}\endNode{}\endPar{}\endNode{}\Node%

{1}}Since mentioning a function's name without parenthesis is enough
        to call it, the '\Node%
{\def\Element%
{259}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}@\endSeq{}\endNode{}' prefix is added before the
        function name to indicate that we want a pointer to it, not to execute
        it. This distinction is justified if you consider that a function
        could itself return a pointer to function, thus you can't let the
        compiler guess whether or not to call the function.\endPar{}\endNode{}\Node%

{1}}\LineField%
{\def\FieldWidth%
{16\p@}\def\FieldWidthFactor%
{0}}1. \endLineField{}The reason for that is simple. Parsing C headers is a
              nightmare. It's already done for typedefs, for example, and
              there are still cases where it fails (e.g. SDL headers).
              Portable header files are usually filled with \#defines, and it
              makes the ooc parser have a hard job understanding them. To fix
              that, we'd have to complete the ooc included C preprocessor,
              which would be very time consuming. For now, you can work around
              the 'unrecognized types' issue with the 'ctype' keyword.\endPar{}\endNode{}\endSeq{}\endNode{}\endSeq{}\endSpS{}\endNode{}\Node%

{1}}For now, ooc has a simple inheritance model, somewhat like Java.
        To inherit a class, use the \Node%
{\def\Element%
{306}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}from\endSeq{}\endNode{} keyword. To
        override a function, use the \Node%
{\def\Element%
{307}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}override\endSeq{}\endNode{} keyword, or
        define a function with the same signature. \Node%
{\def\Element%
{308}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}override\endSeq{}\endNode{}
        has the advantage of change checking: if the signature of the
        implemented function changes, it'll yield a compile error, instead of
        compiling the wrong version silently (it has the same use as the
        \Node%
{\def\Element%
{309}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}@Override\endSeq{}\endNode{} annotation in Java).\endPar{}\endNode{}\Node%

~~~~printf("Quelque-\/chose~ne~tourne~pas~rond.\char92{}n");

~~override~roll~\{
~~~~printf("Pierre~qui~roule~n'amasse~pas~mousse\char92{}n");
~~\}

\}

func~main~\{

~~Wheel~w~=~new;
~~w.roll;~//~prints~"Quelque-\/chose",~etc.

~~BetterWheel~bw~=~new;
~~bw.roll;~//~prints~"Pierre~qui~roule",~etc.

~~Wheel~w2~=~(Wheel)~bw;~//~cast~as~in~C/Java
~~w2.roll;~//~prints~"Pierre~qui~roule",~etc.

        (called default constructor), if there are no constructors in a class.

        disappears.\endPar{}\endNode{}\Node%

{1}}You can declare classes as abstract. They can no longer
        instanciated, and serve as base classes for other. In abstract
        classes, you can define abstract functions, that must be implemented
        by concrete (=non-\/abstract) child classes. To implement an abstract
        function, use the \Node%
{\def\Element%
{321}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}implement\endSeq{}\endNode{} keyword. It has the
        same advantage as the \Node%
{\def\Element%
{322}}\Seq%
{\def\fFamName{Courier-New}\def\fSize%
{9\p@}}override\endSeq{}\endNode{} keyword.\endPar{}\endNode{}\Node%

{1}}import~structs.Array;

abstract~class~Singer~\{

~~abstract~func~sing;

\}

class~Hives~from~Singer~\{

~~implement~sing~\{
~~~~printf("Tick~tick~tick~BOOM!\char92{}n");
~~\}

\}

class~Spiderbait~from~Singer~\{

~~implement~sing~\{
~~~~printf("Tonight,~I'm~going~outta~my~head!\char92{}n");
~~\}

\}

func~main~\{

~~ArrayList~singers~=~new;
~~singers.add(new~Hives);
~~singers.add(new~Spiderbait);

~~for(Singer~singer:~singers)~\{
~~~~singer.sing;
~~\}

\}\endPar{}\endNode{}\endSeq{}\endDisplayGroup{}\endNode{}\Node%

      and you use them, you can get 'Can't resolve type <\/type>\/' compile
      errors. To work around the problem, use the \Node%
{\def\Element%
{425}}\Seq%

{9\p@}}ctype\endSeq{}\endNode{}
      keyword. It simply instructs the ooc compiler that the type is typedef'd
      somewhere in a header, and that it shouldn't worry about it.\endPar{}\endNode{}\Node%

{0}}1. \endLineField{}What is the hassle with Java packages? You have to declare a

        package declaration into each .java source file, corresponding exactly

{\def\Element%
{456}}\Par%

{1}}The 'meta-\/class structure' exists in one copy in memory for each
        defined class. It contains all the information which is shared between
        instances of a class, such as function pointers to the right
        implementation of functions.\endPar{}\endNode{}\Node%

{1}\def\saNom%
{5\p@}\def\saMin%
{5\p@}\def\saMax%
{5\p@}\def\saConditional%
{1}}Since a child class (a class that inherits from a base class)
        only add member variables and functions, its structs begin with
        exactly the same field order as its parent, and then its own member
        variables/functions are added. This allows seamless C casts, which are
        basically free from a performance point of view.\endPar{}\endNode{}\endSeq{}\endDisplayGroup{}\endNode{}\Node%

{\def\Element%
{539}\def\ProcessingMode%
{listitem-content-mode}}\Seq%
{}Java and C\# suffer from the drawbacks of general-\/purpose
        languages running on Virtual Machines. Namely, they tend to be
        bloated, to have heavy runtime libraries, and to be significantly less
        performant than compiled languages in certain situations. However,
        it's true that with JIT (Just-\/In-\/Time) technology, a VM language can
        run almost as fast as a compiled language, in certain situations. See
        the \Node%

{1}}A \Character{8220}Transparent\Character{8221} copy of the Document means a machine-\/readable
    copy, represented in a format whose specification is available to the
    general public, that is suitable for revising the document
    straightforwardly with generic text editors or (for images composed of
    pixels) generic paint programs or (for drawings) some widely available
    drawing editor, and that is suitable for input to text formatters or for
    automatic translation to a variety of formats suitable for input to text
    formatters. A copy made in an otherwise Transparent file format whose
    markup, or absence of markup, has been arranged to thwart or discourage
    subsequent modification by readers is not Transparent. An image format is
    not Transparent if used for any substantial amount of text. A copy that is
    not \Character{8220}Transparent\Character{8221} is called \Character{8220}Opaque\Character{8221}.\endPar{}\endNode{}\Node%
